	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Daylight saving time</h1>

	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>


		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the concept of daylight saving time in general. For local implementations, see <a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time_by_country" title="Daylight saving time by country">Daylight saving time by country</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"DST" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/DST_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="DST (disambiguation)">DST (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Time adjustment practice</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DST_Countries_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="World map. Europe, most of North America, parts of southern South America and southeastern Australia, and a few other places use DST. Most of equatorial Africa and a few other places near the equator have never used DST. The rest of the landmass is marked as formerly using DST." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/DST_Countries_Map.png/330px-DST_Countries_Map.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/DST_Countries_Map.png/495px-DST_Countries_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/DST_Countries_Map.png/660px-DST_Countries_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="4972" data-file-height="2492" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DST_Countries_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Daylight saving time regions: <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #1a80e6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Northern hemisphere summer</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ee690a; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Southern hemisphere summer</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #c6c6c6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Formerly used daylight saving</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7f7f7f; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Never used daylight saving</div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Daylight saving time</b> (<b>DST</b>), also <b>daylight savings time</b> or <b>daylight time</b> (United States and Canada) and <b>summer time</b> (United Kingdom, European Union, and others), is the practice of advancing clocks during warmer months so that darkness falls later each day according to the clock. The typical implementation of DST is to set clocks forward by one hour in the spring ("spring forward") and set clocks back by one hour in autumn ("fall back") to return to standard time.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>  In other words, there is one 23-hour day in late winter or early spring and one 25-hour day in the autumn.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Hudson_(entomologist)" title="George Hudson (entomologist)">George Hudson</a> proposed the idea of daylight saving in 1895.<sup id="cite_ref-DNZB-Hudson_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DNZB-Hudson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> organized the first nationwide implementation starting on April 30, 1916. <a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time_by_country" title="Daylight saving time by country">Many countries</a> have used it at various times since then, particularly since the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">1970s energy crisis</a>. DST is generally not observed near the equator, where sunrise and sunset times do not vary enough to justify it. Some countries observe it only in some regions; for example, parts of Australia observe it, while other parts do not.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Only a minority of the world's population uses DST; Asia and Africa generally do not observe it.
</p><p>DST clock shifts sometimes complicate timekeeping and can disrupt travel, billing, record keeping, medical devices, heavy equipment,<sup id="cite_ref-Neumann_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neumann-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and sleep patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-Lahti_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lahti-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Computer software often adjusts clocks automatically, but policy changes by various jurisdictions of DST dates and timings may be confusing.<sup id="cite_ref-Tong_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tong-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationale">Rationale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Rationale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Industrial_society" title="Industrial society">Industrialized societies</a> usually follow a clock-based schedule for daily activities that do not change throughout the course of the year. The time of day that individuals begin and end work or school, and the coordination of <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">mass transit</a>, for example, usually remain constant year-round. In contrast, an <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_society" title="Agrarian society">agrarian society</a>'s daily routines for work and personal conduct are more likely governed by the length of daylight hours<sup id="cite_ref-Minnesota_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minnesota-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RoSPA_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RoSPA-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Solar_time" title="Solar time">solar time</a>, which change <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">seasonally</a> because of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a>. North and south of the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> daylight lasts longer in summer and shorter in <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a>, with the effect becoming greater the further one moves away from the tropics.
</p><p>By synchronously resetting all clocks in a region to one hour ahead of <a href="/wiki/Standard_time" title="Standard time">standard time</a>, individuals who follow such a year-round schedule will wake an hour earlier than they would have otherwise; they will begin and complete daily work routines an hour earlier, and they will have available to them an extra hour of daylight after their workday activities.<sup id="cite_ref-Hudson_1895_p734_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hudson_1895_p734-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005115–118_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005115–118-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> However, they will have one less hour of daylight at the start of each day, making the policy less practical during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-CRS_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRS-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Handwerk_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handwerk-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the times of sunrise and sunset change at roughly equal rates as the seasons change, proponents of daylight saving time argue that most people prefer a greater increase in daylight hours after the typical <a href="/wiki/Working_time#Workweek_structure" title="Working time">"nine to five"</a> workday.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Willett100_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willett100-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Supporters have also argued that DST decreases <a href="/wiki/Energy_consumption" title="Energy consumption">energy consumption</a> by reducing the need for lighting and heating, but the actual effect on overall energy use is <a href="#Dispute_over_benefits_and_drawbacks">heavily disputed</a>.
</p><p>The manipulation of time at higher latitudes (for example <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>) has little impact on daily life, because the length of day and night changes more extremely throughout the seasons (in comparison to other latitudes), and thus sunrise and sunset times are significantly out of phase with standard working hours regardless of manipulations of the clock.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> DST is also of little use for locations near the equator, because these regions see only a small variation in daylight in the course of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The effect also varies according to how far east or west the location is within its <a href="/wiki/Time_zone" title="Time zone">time zone</a>, with locations farther east inside the time zone benefiting more from DST than locations farther west in the same time zone.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="A water clock. A small human figurine holds a pointer to a cylinder marked by the hours. The cylinder is connected by gears to a water wheel driven by water that also floats, a part that supports the figurine." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg/170px-Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg" decoding="async" width="170" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg/255px-Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg/340px-Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clepsydra-Diagram-Fancy.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ancient <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">water clock</a> that lets hour lengths vary with season.</div></div></div>
<p>Ancient civilizations adjusted daily schedules to the sun more flexibly than DST does, often dividing daylight into 12 hours regardless of daytime, so that each daylight hour became progressively longer during spring and shorter during autumn.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_timekeeping" title="Roman timekeeping">Romans kept time</a> with <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">water clocks</a> that had different scales for different months of the year; at Rome's latitude, the third hour from sunrise (<a href="/wiki/Terce" title="Terce"><i>hora tertia</i></a>) started at 09:02 <a href="/wiki/Solar_time" title="Solar time">solar time</a> and lasted 44 minutes at the winter <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">solstice</a>, but at the summer solstice it started at 06:58 and lasted 75 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> From the 14th century onwards, equal-length civil hours supplanted unequal ones, so <a href="/wiki/Civil_time" title="Civil time">civil time</a> no longer varied by season. Unequal hours are still used in a few traditional settings, such as some monasteries of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and all Jewish ceremonies.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> published the proverb "early to bed and early to rise makes a man healthy, wealthy, and wise",<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and he published a letter in the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_de_Paris" title="Journal de Paris">Journal de Paris</a></i> during his time as an American envoy to France (1776–1785) suggesting that Parisians economize on candles by rising earlier to use morning sunlight.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> This 1784 satire proposed taxing window shutters, rationing candles, and waking the public by ringing church bells and firing cannons at sunrise.<sup id="cite_ref-Franklin_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Despite common misconception, Franklin did not actually propose DST; 18th-century Europe did not even keep precise schedules. However, this changed as rail transport and communication networks required a standardization of time unknown in Franklin's day.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1810, the Spanish National Assembly <a href="/wiki/Cortes_of_C%C3%A1diz" title="Cortes of Cádiz">Cortes of Cádiz</a> issued a regulation that moved certain meeting times forward by one hour from May 1 to September 30 in recognition of seasonal changes, but it did not actually change the clocks. It also acknowledged that private businesses were in the practice of changing their opening hours to suit daylight conditions, but they did so of their own volition.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="Fuzzy head-and-shoulders photo of a 40-year-old man with a mustache." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg/170px-George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg" decoding="async" width="170" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg/255px-George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg/340px-George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1184" data-file-height="1928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George-Vernon-Hudson-RSNZ.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_Hudson_(entomologist)" title="George Hudson (entomologist)">George Hudson</a> invented modern DST, proposing it first in 1895.</div></div></div>
<p>New Zealand entomologist <a href="/wiki/George_Hudson_(entomologist)" title="George Hudson (entomologist)">George Hudson</a> first proposed modern DST. His shift-work job gave him leisure time to collect insects and led him to value after-hours daylight.<sup id="cite_ref-DNZB-Hudson_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DNZB-Hudson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In 1895, he presented a paper to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_New_Zealand#Regional_Constituent_Organisations" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Society of New Zealand">Wellington Philosophical Society</a> proposing a two-hour daylight-saving shift,<sup id="cite_ref-Hudson_1895_p734_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hudson_1895_p734-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and considerable interest was expressed in <a href="/wiki/Christchurch" title="Christchurch">Christchurch</a>; he followed up with an 1898 paper.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Many publications credit the DST proposal to prominent English builder and outdoorsman <a href="/wiki/William_Willett" title="William Willett">William Willett</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> who independently conceived DST in 1905 during a pre-breakfast ride when he observed how many Londoners slept through a large part of a summer day.<sup id="cite_ref-Willett100_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willett100-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Willett also was an avid golfer who disliked cutting short his round at dusk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;20053_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;20053-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> His solution was to advance the clock during the summer months, and he published the proposal two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Willett_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willett-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Liberal Party member of parliament <a href="/wiki/Robert_Pearce_(British_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Pearce (British politician)">Robert Pearce</a> took up the proposal, introducing the first Daylight Saving Bill to the House of Commons on February 12, 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> A select committee was set up to examine the issue, but Pearce's bill did not become law and several other bills failed in the following years. Willett lobbied for the proposal in the UK until his death in 1915.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Port_Arthur,_Ontario" title="Port Arthur, Ontario">Port Arthur, Ontario</a>, Canada was the first city in the world to enact DST, on July 1, 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> This was followed by <a href="/wiki/Orillia" title="Orillia">Orillia</a>, Ontario, introduced by William Sword Frost while mayor from 1911 to 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The first states to adopt DST (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de"><a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sommerzeit" class="extiw" title="de:Sommerzeit">Sommerzeit</a></i>) nationally were those of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and its <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> ally <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> commencing April 30, 1916, as a way to conserve coal during wartime. Britain, most of its <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">allies</a>, and many European neutrals soon followed. Russia and a few other countries waited until the next year, and the United States adopted daylight saving in 1918. Most jurisdictions abandoned DST in the years after the war ended in 1918, with exceptions including Canada, the UK, France, Ireland, and the United States. It became common during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and was widely adopted in America and Europe from the 1970s as a result of the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">1970s energy crisis</a>. Since then, the world has seen many enactments, adjustments, and repeals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200551–89_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200551–89-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
For specific details, see <a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time_by_country" title="Daylight saving time by country">Daylight saving time by country</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Procedure">Procedure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Procedure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time_by_country" title="Daylight saving time by country">Daylight saving time by country</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption-center{text-align:center;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:169px;max-width:169px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:167px;max-width:167px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Begin_CEST.svg" class="image"><img alt="Diagram of a clock showing a transition from 02:00 to 03:00" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Begin_CEST.svg/165px-Begin_CEST.svg.png" decoding="async" width="165" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Begin_CEST.svg/248px-Begin_CEST.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Begin_CEST.svg/330px-Begin_CEST.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="250" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">When DST begins, clocks are advanced by one hour (as if to skip one hour) during the very early morning at the beginning of DST.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:167px;max-width:167px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:End_CEST.svg" class="image"><img alt="Diagram of a clock showing a transition from 03:00 to 02:00" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/End_CEST.svg/165px-End_CEST.svg.png" decoding="async" width="165" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/End_CEST.svg/248px-End_CEST.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/End_CEST.svg/330px-End_CEST.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="250" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">When DST ends, clocks are set back (as if to repeat one hour) during the very early morning. Specific times vary by jurisdiction.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The relevant authorities usually schedule clock shifts for, or soon after, midnight and on a weekend to lessen disruption to weekday schedules.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> A one-hour shift is customary, but twenty-minute and two-hour shifts have been used in the past. In all countries that use daylight saving, the clock is advanced in spring and set back in autumn; the spring change reduces the length of that day and the autumn change increases it. For a midnight shift in spring, a digital display of local time would appear to jump from 23:59:59.9 to 01:00:00.0. For the same clock in autumn, the local time would appear to repeat the hour preceding midnight, i.e. jumping from 23:59:59.9 to 23:00:00.0.
</p><p>In most countries that use daylight saving time, the time applied in the winter is considered as "standard" time and the shift is considered to be a positive offset. The opposite is also possible, with Ireland, for example, defining its summer time to be the legal standard and <a href="/wiki/Winter_time_(clock_lag)" title="Winter time (clock lag)">winter time</a> to be a negative offset.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Morocco sets its clocks back one hour during the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan_(calendar_month)" title="Ramadan (calendar month)">Ramadan</a> relative to its standard time.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The time at which to change clocks differs across jurisdictions. Members of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> conduct a coordinated shift, shifting all zones at the same instant, at 01:00 <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">Coordinated Universal Time</a> (UTC), which means that it changes at 02:00 <a href="/wiki/Central_European_Time" title="Central European Time">Central European Time</a> (CET), equivalent to 03:00 <a href="/wiki/Eastern_European_Time" title="Eastern European Time">Eastern European Time</a> (EET); as a result, the time differences across European time zones remain constant.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Myers_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> North America shifts at 02:00 but at the local time and is consequently uncoordinated—so that, for example, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Time_Zone" title="Mountain Time Zone">Mountain Time</a> is, for one hour in the autumn, zero hours ahead of <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Time_Zone" title="Pacific Time Zone">Pacific Time</a> instead of one hour ahead and, for one hour in the spring, two hours ahead of Pacific Time instead of one hour ahead.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Time-differences-brz-us.png" class="image"><img alt="Time graph. The horizontal axis shows dates in 2008. The vertical axis shows the UTC offsets of eastern Brazil and eastern U.S. In 2008, the difference between the two starts at 3 hours, then goes to 2 hours on February 17 at 24:00 Brazil eastern time, then goes to 1 hour on March 9 at 02:00 U.S. eastern time." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Time-differences-brz-us.png/220px-Time-differences-brz-us.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Time-differences-brz-us.png/330px-Time-differences-brz-us.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Time-differences-brz-us.png/440px-Time-differences-brz-us.png 2x" data-file-width="2032" data-file-height="940" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Time-differences-brz-us.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In early 2008, central Brazil was one, two, or three hours ahead of the eastern United States, depending on the date.</div></div></div>
<p>The dates on which clocks shift also vary with location and year; consequently, the time differences between regions also vary throughout the year. For example, Central European Time is usually six hours ahead of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">North American Eastern Time</a>, except for a few weeks in March and October/November, while the United Kingdom and mainland Chile could be five hours apart during the northern summer, three hours during the southern summer, and four hours for a few weeks per year. Since 1996, <a href="/wiki/Summer_time_in_Europe" title="Summer time in Europe">European Summer Time</a> has been observed from the last Sunday in March to the last Sunday in October; previously the rules were not uniform across the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Starting in 2007, most of the United States and Canada observed DST from the second Sunday in March to the first Sunday in November, almost two-thirds of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, the beginning and ending dates are roughly reversed between the northern and southern hemispheres because spring and autumn are displaced six months. For example, mainland <a href="/wiki/Time_in_Chile" title="Time in Chile">Chile</a> observes DST from the second Saturday in October to the second Saturday in March, with transitions at <a href="/wiki/24-hour_clock#Midnight_00:00_and_24:00" title="24-hour clock">24:00</a> local time.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In some countries time is governed by regional jurisdictions within the country such that some jurisdictions shift and others do not; this is currently the case in Australia, Canada, Mexico, and the United States (formerly in Brazil, etc.).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005179–180_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005179–180-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From year to year, the shift dates may change for political and social reasons. The 2007 U.S. change conformed to the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_2005" title="Energy Policy Act of 2005">Energy Policy Act of 2005</a>; previously, from 1987 through 2006, the start- and end-dates were the first Sunday in April and the last Sunday in October. From 2008, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> has had the right to go back to the previous dates.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Proponents for permanently retaining November as the month for ending DST point to <a href="/wiki/Halloween" title="Halloween">Halloween</a> as a reason to delay the change—to provide extra daylight on October 31. For example, in the United States in 2019, daylight-saving time began at 2:00 am local time on March 10, and ended at 2:00 am Local Time on November 3. In the past, <a href="/wiki/Australia_Central_Daylight_Time" class="mw-redirect" title="Australia Central Daylight Time">Australian</a> state jurisdictions not only changed at different local times but sometimes on different dates; for example, in 2008 most DST-observing states shifted clocks forward on October 5 but <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> shifted on October 26.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Daylight saving has caused controversy since it began.<sup id="cite_ref-Downing-Prerau_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Downing-Prerau-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> argued that it enlarges "the opportunities for the pursuit of health and happiness among the millions of people who live in this country"<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and pundits have dubbed it "Daylight Slaving Time".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005117_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005117-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Retailing, sports, and tourism interests have historically favored daylight saving, while agricultural and evening entertainment interests have opposed it; its initial adoption was prompted by energy crises and war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005xi_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005xi-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The fate of Willett's 1907 proposal illustrates several political issues. It attracted many supporters, including <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, Churchill, <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">Edward VII</a> (who used half-hour DST at Sandringham or "<a href="/wiki/Sandringham_time" title="Sandringham time">Sandringham time</a>"), the managing director of <a href="/wiki/Harrods" title="Harrods">Harrods</a>, and the manager of the National Bank. However, the opposition was stronger, including Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Christie_(astronomer)" title="William Christie (astronomer)">William Christie</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Royal" title="Astronomer Royal">Astronomer Royal</a>), <a href="/wiki/George_Darwin" title="George Darwin">George Darwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Napier_Shaw" title="Napier Shaw">Napier Shaw</a> (director of the Meteorological Office), many agricultural organizations, and theatre owners. After many hearings, the proposal was narrowly defeated in a parliamentary committee vote in 1909. Willett's allies introduced similar bills every year from 1911 through 1914, to no avail.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200512–24_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200512–24-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The U.S. was even more skeptical; <a href="/wiki/Andrew_James_Peters" title="Andrew James Peters">Andrew Peters</a> introduced a DST bill to the House of Representatives in May 1909, but it soon died in committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200572–73_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200572–73-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="Poster titled &quot;VICTORY! CONGRESS PASSES DAYLIGHT SAVING BILL&quot; showing Uncle Sam turning a clock to daylight saving time as a clock-headed figure throws his hat in the air. The clock face of the figure reads &quot;ONE HOUR OF EXTRA DAYLIGHT&quot;. The bottom caption says &quot;Get Your Hoe Ready!&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg/170px-Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg" decoding="async" width="170" height="302" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg/255px-Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg/340px-Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2365" data-file-height="4200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victory-Cigar-Congress-Passes-DST.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Retailers generally favor DST; <a href="/wiki/United_Cigar_Stores" title="United Cigar Stores">United Cigar Stores</a> hailed a 1918 DST bill</div></div></div>
<p>Germany led the way by starting DST (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de"><a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sommerzeit" class="extiw" title="de:Sommerzeit">Sommerzeit</a></i>) during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> on April 30, 1916 together with <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">its allies</a> to alleviate hardships from wartime coal shortages and air raid blackouts. The political equation changed in other countries; the United Kingdom used DST first on May 21, 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200551–70_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200551–70-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> U.S. retailing and manufacturing interests led by <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> industrialist Robert Garland soon began lobbying for DST, but they were opposed by railroads. The U.S.'s 1917 entry to the war overcame objections, and DST was established in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200580–101_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200580–101-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The war's end swung the pendulum back. Farmers continued to dislike DST, and many countries repealed it after the war, like Germany itself who dropped DST from 1919 to 1939 and from 1950 to 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Britain was an exception; it retained DST nationwide but adjusted transition dates over the years for several reasons, including special rules during the 1920s and 1930s to avoid clock shifts on Easter mornings. Now summer time begins annually on the last Sunday in March under a European Community directive, which may be Easter Sunday (as in 2016).<sup id="cite_ref-Myers_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The U.S. was more typical; Congress repealed DST after 1919. President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> was also an avid golfer like Willet, and he vetoed the repeal twice but his second veto was overridden.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005103–110_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005103–110-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Only a few U.S. cities retained DST locally,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> including New York so that its financial exchanges could maintain an hour of arbitrage trading with London, and Chicago and <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a> to keep pace with New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Spring_Forward&#39;&#39;200547–48_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Spring_Forward&#39;&#39;200547–48-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Wilson's successor <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> opposed DST as a "deception", reasoning that people should instead get up and go to work earlier in the summer. He ordered District of Columbia federal employees to start work at 8 am rather than 9 am during the summer of 1922. Some businesses followed suit, though many others did not; the experiment was not repeated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005115–118_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005115–118-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since Germany's adoption in 1916, the world has seen many enactments, adjustments, and repeals of DST, with similar politics involved.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/History_of_time_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="History of time in the United States">history of time in the United States</a> includes DST during both world wars, but no standardization of peacetime DST until 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005147–155,_175–180_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005147–155,_175–180-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St._Paul,_Minnesota" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Paul, Minnesota">St. Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis,_Minnesota" class="mw-redirect" title="Minneapolis, Minnesota">Minneapolis, Minnesota</a>, were on different times for two weeks in May 1965 when the capital city decided to join most of the nation by starting Daylight Saving Time, while Minneapolis opted to follow the later date set by state law.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the mid-1980s, <a href="/wiki/Clorox" title="Clorox">Clorox</a> and <a href="/wiki/7-Eleven" title="7-Eleven">7-Eleven</a> provided the primary funding for the Daylight Saving Time Coalition behind the 1987 extension to U.S. DST. Both senators from <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Craig" title="Larry Craig">Larry Craig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mike_Crapo" title="Mike Crapo">Mike Crapo</a>, voted for it based on the premise that fast-food restaurants sell more French fries during DST, which are made from Idaho potatoes.<sup id="cite_ref-Benfield_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benfield-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/1992_Queensland_daylight_saving_referendum" title="1992 Queensland daylight saving referendum">referendum on daylight saving</a> was held in <a href="/wiki/Queensland,_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Queensland, Australia">Queensland, Australia</a>, in 1992, after a three-year trial of daylight saving. It was defeated with a 54.5% "no" vote, with regional and rural areas strongly opposed, while those in the metropolitan southeast were in favor.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, the Sporting Goods Manufacturers Association and the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Convenience_Stores" title="National Association of Convenience Stores">National Association of Convenience Stores</a> successfully lobbied for the 2007 extension to U.S. DST.<sup id="cite_ref-Beam_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beam-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In December 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Daylight_Saving_for_South_East_Queensland" title="Daylight Saving for South East Queensland">Daylight Saving for South East Queensland</a> (DS4SEQ) political party was officially registered in Queensland, advocating the implementation of a dual-time zone arrangement for daylight saving in <a href="/wiki/South_East_Queensland" title="South East Queensland">South East Queensland</a>, while the rest of the state maintains standard time.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> DS4SEQ contested the March 2009 Queensland state election with 32 candidates and received one percent of the statewide primary vote, equating to around 2.5% across the 32 electorates contested.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> After a three-year trial, more than 55% of Western Australians voted against DST in 2009, with rural areas strongly opposed.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Queensland Independent member <a href="/wiki/Peter_Wellington" title="Peter Wellington">Peter Wellington</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Daylight_Saving_for_South_East_Queensland_Referendum_Act_2010" class="mw-redirect" title="Daylight Saving for South East Queensland Referendum Act 2010">Daylight Saving for South East Queensland Referendum Bill 2010</a> into the Queensland parliament on April 14, 2010, after being approached by the DS4SEQ political party, calling for a referendum at the next state election on the introduction of daylight saving into South East Queensland under a dual-time zone arrangement.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Bill was defeated in the Queensland parliament on June 15, 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the UK, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Prevention_of_Accidents" title="Royal Society for the Prevention of Accidents">Royal Society for the Prevention of Accidents</a> supports a proposal to observe SDST's additional hour year-round, but that is opposed in some industries, such as postal workers and farmers, and particularly by those living in the northern regions of the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-RoSPA_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RoSPA-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In some Muslim countries, DST is temporarily abandoned during <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> (the month when no food should be eaten between sunrise and sunset), since the DST would delay the evening dinner. Iran maintains DST during Ramadan,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> but most Muslim countries do not use DST, partially for this reason.
</p><p>Russia declared in 2011 that it would stay in DST all year long, followed by a similar declaration from <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Russia's plan generated widespread complaints due to the dark of winter time morning, and thus was abandoned in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The country changed its clocks to Standard Time on October 26, 2014 and intends to stay there permanently.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispute_over_benefits_and_drawbacks">Dispute over benefits and drawbacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dispute over benefits and drawbacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William-Willett.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A standing man in three-piece suit, facing camera. He is about 60 and is bald with a mustache. His left hand is in his pants pocket, and his right hand is in front of his chest, holding his pocket watch." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/William-Willett.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="248" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="120" data-file-height="248" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William-Willett.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Willett" title="William Willett">William Willett</a> independently proposed DST in 1907 and advocated it tirelessly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200522_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200522-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Proponents of DST generally argue that it saves energy, promotes outdoor leisure activity in the evening (in summer), and is therefore good for physical and psychological health, reduces traffic accidents, reduces crime or is good for business.
</p><p>Opponents argue that DST disrupts our circadian rhythms, negatively impacting our health,<sup id="cite_ref-Roenneberg_June_2019_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roenneberg_June_2019-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roenneberg_August_2019_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roenneberg_August_2019-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> that it increases fatal traffic collisions,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> that the actual energy savings are inconclusive,<sup id="cite_ref-NatGeo_Time_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_Time-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and that DST increases health risks such as heart attack.<sup id="cite_ref-NatGeo_Time_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_Time-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Farmers have tended to oppose DST.<sup id="cite_ref-Minnesota2_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minnesota2-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Having a common agreement about the day's layout or schedule has so many advantages that a standard schedule over whole countries or large areas has generally been chosen over efforts in which some people get up earlier and others do not.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The advantages of coordination are so great that many people ignore whether DST is in effect by altering their work schedules to coordinate with television broadcasts or daylight.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> DST is commonly not observed during most of winter, because the days are shorter then; workers may have no sunlit leisure time, and students may need to leave for school in the dark.<sup id="cite_ref-CRS_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRS-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Since DST is applied to many varying communities, its effects may be very different depending on their culture, light levels, geography, and climate.  Because of this variation, it is hard to make generalized conclusions about the effects of the practice.  The costs and benefits may differ between places.  Some areas may adopt DST simply as a matter of coordination with other areas rather than for any other benefits.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_use">Energy use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Energy use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Subtropical.png" class="image"><img alt="A map of the world, with subtropical climates highlighted" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Subtropical.png/280px-Subtropical.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Subtropical.png/420px-Subtropical.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Subtropical.png/560px-Subtropical.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="628" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Subtropical.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Subtropical climates are highlighted in yellow on this map. People living in these areas may consume more energy as a result of DST.</div></div></div>
<p>A 2017 meta-analysis of 44 studies found that DST leads to electricity savings of 0.3% during the days when DST applies.<sup id="cite_ref-Havranek_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Havranek-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irsova_-_Doesn&#39;t_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irsova_-_Doesn&#39;t-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The meta-analysis furthermore found that "electricity savings are larger for countries farther away from the equator, while subtropical regions consume more electricity because of DST."<sup id="cite_ref-Havranek_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Havranek-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irsova_-_Doesn&#39;t_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irsova_-_Doesn&#39;t-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>  This means that DST may conserve electricity in some countries, such as Canada and the United Kingdom, but be wasteful in other places, such as Mexico, the southern United States, and northern Africa.  The savings in electricity may also be offset by extra use of other types of energy, such as heating fuel.
</p><p>The period of daylight saving time before the longest day is shorter than the period after, in several countries including the United States and Europe. For example, in the U.S. the period of daylight saving time is defined by the Energy Policy Act of 2005. The period for daylight saving time was extended by changing the start date from the first Sunday of April to the second Sunday of March, and the end date from the last Sunday in October to the first Sunday in November.
</p><p>DST's potential to save energy comes primarily from its effects on residential lighting, which consumes about 3.5% of electricity in the United States and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-Aries_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aries-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> (For comparison, air conditioning uses 16.5% of energy in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>)  Delaying the nominal time of sunset and sunrise reduces the use of artificial light in the evening and increases it in the morning. As Franklin's 1784 satire pointed out, lighting costs are reduced if the evening reduction outweighs the morning increase, as in high-latitude summer when most people wake up well after sunrise. An early goal of DST was to reduce evening usage of <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_lighting" class="mw-redirect" title="Incandescent lighting">incandescent lighting</a>, once a primary use of electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Although energy conservation remains an important goal,<sup id="cite_ref-Ahuja_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahuja-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> energy usage patterns have greatly changed since then. Electricity use is greatly affected by geography, climate, and economics, so the results of a study conducted in one place may not be relevant to another country or climate.<sup id="cite_ref-Aries_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aries-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>In the United States, research indicates that DST reduces residential lighting costs but usually increases total energy consumption, especially when non-electricity sources of energy consumption are considered.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>  These non-electricity sources of energy consumption include extra heating fuel on the colder, darker mornings and extra gasoline used to drive to shopping and sporting activities.  In some cases, DST increases residential electricity consumption, such as when people use more <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> in the longer, hotter evenings.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>A 2007 study estimated that winter daylight saving would prevent a 2% increase in average daily electricity consumption in Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> This paper was revised in October 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2000, when parts of <a href="/wiki/Time_in_Australia" title="Time in Australia">Australia</a> began DST in late winter, overall electricity consumption did not change, but the morning peak load and prices increased.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  The overall consumption was the same because people used more electricity in the dark mornings, and correspondingly less electricity in the brighter evenings.  In <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> during summer 2006–2007, DST increased electricity consumption during hotter days and decreased it during cooler days, with consumption rising 0.6% overall.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Although a 2007 study estimated that introducing DST to Japan would reduce household lighting energy consumption,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> a 2007 simulation estimated that DST would increase overall energy use in <a href="/wiki/Osaka" title="Osaka">Osaka</a> residences by 0.1%, with a &lt;0.1% decrease due to less lighting more than outweighed by a 0.2% increase due to extra cooling; neither study examined non-residential energy use.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> This is probably because DST's effect on lighting energy use is mainly noticeable in residences.<sup id="cite_ref-Aries_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aries-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Several studies have suggested that DST increases motor fuel consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Aries_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aries-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The 2008 DOE report found no significant increase in motor gasoline consumption due to the 2007 United States extension of DST.<sup id="cite_ref-DOE-2008_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-2008-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_effects">Economic effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Economic effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Those who benefit most from DST are the retailers, sporting goods makers, and other businesses that benefit from extra afternoon sunlight.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>  Having more hours of sunlight in between the end of the typical workday and bedtime induces customers to shop and to participate in outdoor afternoon sports.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>  People are more likely to stop by a store on their way home from work if the sun is still up.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>  In 1984, <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine estimated that a seven-week extension of DST would yield an additional $30&#160;million for <a href="/wiki/7-Eleven" title="7-Eleven">7-Eleven</a> stores, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Golf_Foundation" title="National Golf Foundation">National Golf Foundation</a> estimated the extension would increase golf industry revenues $200&#160;million to $300&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Spring_Forward&#39;&#39;2005147–148_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Spring_Forward&#39;&#39;2005147–148-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> A 1999 study estimated that DST increases the revenue of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s leisure sector by about 3%.<sup id="cite_ref-Aries_89-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aries-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Conversely, DST can harm some farmers,<sup id="cite_ref-NatGeo_Time_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_Time-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NFU_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NFU-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> young children, who have difficulty getting enough sleep at night when the evenings are bright,<sup id="cite_ref-NatGeo_Time_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_Time-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and others whose hours are set by the sun.<sup id="cite_ref-farming_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-farming-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> One reason why farmers oppose DST is that grain is best harvested after dew evaporates, so when field hands arrive and leave earlier in summer, their labor is less valuable.<sup id="cite_ref-Minnesota_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minnesota-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Dairy farmers are another group who complain of the change. Their cows are sensitive to the timing of milking, so delivering milk earlier disrupts their systems.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Today some farmers' groups are in favor of DST.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>DST also hurts prime-time television broadcast ratings,<sup id="cite_ref-Kissell_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kissell-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NatGeo_Time_82-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_Time-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Drive-in_theater" title="Drive-in theater">drive-ins</a> and other theaters.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Changing clocks and DST rules has a direct economic cost, entailing extra work to support remote meetings, computer applications and the like. For example, a 2007 North American rule change cost an estimated $500&#160;million to $1&#160;billion,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Utah_State_University" title="Utah State University">Utah State University</a> economist William F. Shughart II has estimated the lost opportunity cost at around US$1.7&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-NatGeo_Time_82-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_Time-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Although it has been argued that clock shifts correlate with decreased <a href="/wiki/Economic_efficiency" title="Economic efficiency">economic efficiency</a>, and that in 2000 the daylight-saving effect implied an estimated one-day loss of $31&#160;billion on U.S. stock exchanges,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> the estimated numbers depend on the methodology.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The results have been disputed,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and the original authors have refuted the points raised by disputers.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_safety">Public safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Public safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1975 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">United States Department of Transportation</a> (DOT) conservatively identified a 0.7% reduction in traffic fatalities during DST, and estimated the real reduction at 1.5% to 2.0%,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> but the 1976 NBS review of the DOT study found no differences in traffic fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-CRS_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRS-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1995 the <a href="/wiki/Insurance_Institute_for_Highway_Safety" title="Insurance Institute for Highway Safety">Insurance Institute for Highway Safety</a> estimated a reduction of 1.2%, including a 5.0% reduction in crashes fatal to pedestrians.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Others have found similar reductions.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Single/Double Summer Time (SDST), a variant where clocks are one hour ahead of the sun in winter and two in summer, has been projected to reduce traffic fatalities by 3% to 4% in the UK, compared to ordinary DST.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> However, accidents do increase by as much as 11% during the two weeks that follow the end of British Summer Time.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> It is not clear whether sleep disruption contributes to fatal accidents immediately after the spring clock shifts.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> A correlation between clock shifts and traffic accidents has been observed in North America and the UK but not in Finland or Sweden. Four reports have found that this effect is smaller than the overall reduction in traffic fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> A 2009 U.S. study found that on Mondays after the switch to DST, workers sleep an average of 40 minutes less, and are injured at work more often and more severely.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>DST likely reduces some kinds of crime, such as robbery and sexual assault, as fewer potential victims are outdoors after dusk.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>  Artificial outdoor lighting has a marginal and sometimes even contradictory influence on crime and fear of crime.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In several countries, fire safety officials encourage citizens to use the two annual clock shifts as reminders to replace batteries in <a href="/wiki/Smoke_detector" title="Smoke detector">smoke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_detector" title="Carbon monoxide detector">carbon monoxide detectors</a>, particularly in autumn, just before the heating and candle season causes an increase in home fires. Similar twice-yearly tasks include reviewing and practicing fire escape and family disaster plans, inspecting vehicle lights, checking storage areas for hazardous materials, reprogramming thermostats, and seasonal vaccinations.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Locations without DST can instead use the first days of spring and autumn as reminders.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 study in the <i><a href="/wiki/American_Economic_Journal" title="American Economic Journal">American Economic Journal: Applied Economics</a></i> estimated that "the transition into DST caused over 30 deaths at a social cost of $275 million annually," primarily by increasing sleep deprivation.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Using a large US database of 732,835 fatal motor vehicle accidents (MVA) recorded from 1996 to 2017, Fritz et al. (2019) found a 6% increase in fatal MVA risk in the workweek following the spring transition to DST, which was more pronounced in the morning and further West of a time zone. There were no effects of the fall-back transition to Standard Time (ST) on MVA risk, supporting the hypothesis that circadian misalignment and sleep deprivation underlie MVA risk increases.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png" class="image"><img alt="Graph of sunrise and sunset times for 2007. The horizontal axis is the date; the vertical axis is the times of sunset and sunrise. There is a bulge in the centre during summer, when sunrise is early and sunset late. There are step functions in spring and fall, when DST starts and stops." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png/280px-Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png/420px-Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png/560px-Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png 2x" data-file-width="851" data-file-height="669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_GB_DaylightChart.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clock shifts affecting apparent sunrise and sunset times at <a href="/wiki/Greenwich" title="Greenwich">Greenwich</a> in 2007<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SpringFwd-FallBack.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/SpringFwd-FallBack.png/280px-SpringFwd-FallBack.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/SpringFwd-FallBack.png/420px-SpringFwd-FallBack.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/SpringFwd-FallBack.png/560px-SpringFwd-FallBack.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SpringFwd-FallBack.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Justification for Daylight Saving Time to the effect that it is a more natural adjustment for people rising with the sun</div></div></div>
<p>Experts in circadian rhythms and sleep are warning about the negative health implications of DST.  DST cuts on sleep and causes an increased mismatch between the body clock and local time, a condition called social jetlag. Both sleep deprivation and social jetlag have been associated with negative effects on physical and mental health outcomes, including increased risks for diabetes, obesity, heart disease, depression, and some forms of cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-Roenneberg_June_2019_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roenneberg_June_2019-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roenneberg_August_2019_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roenneberg_August_2019-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Year-round Standard Time (not year-round DST) is proposed to be the preferred option for public health and safety.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Globe_and_Mail_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Globe_and_Mail-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Sacramento_Bee_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Sacramento_Bee-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-U_Calgary_News_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-U_Calgary_News-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CBC_News_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBC_News-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In societies with fixed work schedules it provides more afternoon sunlight for outdoor exercise.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> It alters sunlight exposure; the effects depend on one's location and daily schedule, as sunlight triggers <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> synthesis in the skin, but overexposure can lead to skin cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> DST may help in <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a> by causing individuals to rise earlier,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> but some argue the reverse.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Retinitis_Pigmentosa" class="mw-redirect" title="Retinitis Pigmentosa">Retinitis Pigmentosa</a> Foundation Fighting Blindness, chaired by blind sports magnate <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Gund" title="Gordon Gund">Gordon Gund</a>, successfully lobbied in 1985 and 2005 for U.S. DST extensions.<sup id="cite_ref-Benfield_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benfield-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beam_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beam-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> DST shifts are associated with higher rates of <a href="/wiki/Ischemic_stroke" class="mw-redirect" title="Ischemic stroke">ischemic stroke</a> in the first two days after the shift, though not in the week thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Clock shifts were found to increase the risk of heart attack by 10 percent,<sup id="cite_ref-NatGeo_Time_82-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_Time-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and to disrupt sleep and reduce its efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-Lahti_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lahti-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Effects on seasonal adaptation of the <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">circadian rhythm</a> can be severe and last for weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> A 2008 study found that although male suicide rates rise in the weeks after the spring transition, the relationship weakened greatly after adjusting for season.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> A 2008 Swedish study found that heart attacks were significantly more common the first three weekdays after the spring transition, and significantly less common the first weekday after the autumn transition.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> A 2013 review found little evidence that people slept more on the night after the fall DST shift, even though it is often described as allowing people to sleep for an hour longer than normal. The same review stated that the lost hour of sleep resulting from the spring shift appears to result in sleep loss for at least a week afterward.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The government of <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> cited health complications due to clock shifts as a reason for abolishing DST in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In March 2011, <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Medvedev" class="mw-redirect" title="Dmitri Medvedev">Dmitri Medvedev</a>, president of Russia, claimed that "stress of changing clocks" was the motivation for Russia to stay in DST all year long. Officials at the time talked about an annual increase in suicides.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An unexpected adverse effect of daylight saving time may lie in the fact that an extra part of morning rush hour traffic occurs before dawn and traffic emissions then cause higher air pollution than during daylight hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckx_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckx-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> reported that judges who experienced sleep deprivation as a result of DST tended to issue longer sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complexity_and_disadvantages">Complexity and disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Complexity and disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>DST's clock shifts have the obvious disadvantage of complexity. People must remember to change their clocks; this can be time-consuming, particularly for mechanical clocks that cannot be moved backward safely.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> People who work across time zone boundaries need to keep track of multiple DST rules, as not all locations observe DST or observe it the same way. The length of the calendar day becomes variable; it is no longer always 24 hours. Disruption to meetings, travel, broadcasts, billing systems, and records management is common, and can be expensive.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> During an autumn transition from 02:00 to 01:00, a clock reads times from 01:00:00 through 01:59:59 twice, possibly leading to confusion.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lists of time zones and time differences usually do not include daylight saving time, as that is considered complicated and would mean different times over the seasons of the year.
