For example, UK is usually listed as GMT±0 and Japan as GMT+9 and Sydney, Australia, as GMT+10. But in January, Sydney has GMT+11 and in July, UK has GMT+1, so the differences between all those countries vary during the year. Since lists avoid taking complicated daylight saving time into account, they give wrong information about actual time.
</p><p>Damage to a German steel facility occurred during a DST transition in 1993, when a computer timing system linked to a radio time synchronization signal allowed molten steel to cool for one hour less than the required duration, resulting in spattering of molten steel when it was poured.<sup id="cite_ref-Neumann_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neumann-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Medical devices may generate adverse events that could harm patients, without being obvious to clinicians responsible for care.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> These problems are compounded when the DST rules themselves change; software developers must test and perhaps modify many programs, and users must install updates and restart applications. Consumers must update devices such as programmable thermostats with the correct DST rules or manually adjust the devices' clocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Tong_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tong-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A common strategy to resolve these problems in computer systems is to express time using the <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">Coordinated Universal Time</a> (UTC; not the same as <a href="/wiki/Time_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Time in the United Kingdom">London time</a>) rather than the local time zone. For example, <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a>-based computer systems use the UTC-based <a href="/wiki/Unix_time" title="Unix time">Unix time</a> internally.
</p><p>Some clock-shift problems could be avoided by adjusting clocks continuously<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> or at least more gradually<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>—for example, Willett at first suggested weekly 20-minute transitions—but this would add complexity and has never been implemented.
</p><p>DST inherits and can magnify the disadvantages of <a href="/wiki/Standard_time" title="Standard time">standard time</a>. For example, when reading a <a href="/wiki/Sundial" title="Sundial">sundial</a>, one must compensate for it along with time zone and natural discrepancies.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Also, sun-exposure guidelines such as avoiding the sun within two hours of noon become less accurate when DST is in effect.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology"><span id="Mnemonic"></span>Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As explained by Richard Meade in the English Journal of the (American) National Council of Teachers of English, the form <i>daylight savings time</i> (with an "s") was already in 1978 much more common than the older form <i>daylight saving time</i> in American English ("the change has been virtually accomplished"). Nevertheless, even dictionaries such as Merriam-Webster's, American Heritage, and Oxford, which describe actual usage instead of prescribing outdated usage (and therefore also list the newer form), still list the older form first. This is because the older form is still very common in print and preferred by many editors. ("Although <i>daylight saving time</i> is considered correct, <i>daylight savings time</i> (with an "s") is commonly used.")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005xv_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;2005xv-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The first two words are sometimes hyphenated (<i>daylight-saving(s) time</i>). Merriam-Webster's also lists the forms <b>daylight saving</b> (without "time"), <b>daylight savings</b> (without "time"), and <b>daylight time</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-variants_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-variants-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The Oxford Dictionary of American Usage and Style explains the development and current situation as follows: "Although the singular form <i>daylight saving time</i> is the original one, dating from the early 20th century—and is preferred by some usage critics—the plural form is now extremely common in AmE. [...] The rise of <i>daylight savings time</i> appears to have resulted from the avoidance of a miscue: when <i>saving</i> is used, readers might puzzle momentarily over whether <i>saving</i> is a gerund (the saving of daylight) or a participle (the time for saving). [...] Using <i>savings</i> as the adjective—as in <i>savings account</i> or <i>savings bond</i>—makes perfect sense.  More than that, it ought to be accepted as the better form."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Britain, Willett's 1907 proposal<sup id="cite_ref-Willett_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willett-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> used the term <i>daylight saving</i>, but by 1911 the term <i>summer time</i> replaced <i>daylight saving time</i> in draft legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200522_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200522-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The same or similar expressions are used in many other languages: <i>Sommerzeit</i> in German, <i>zomertijd</i> in Dutch, <i>kesäaika</i> in Finnish, <i>horario de verano</i> or <i>hora de verano</i> in Spanish, and <i><a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/heure_d%27%C3%A9t%C3%A9" class="extiw" title="fr:heure d&#39;été">heure d'été</a></i> in French.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200551–70_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Seize_the_Daylight&#39;&#39;200551–70-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The name of local time typically changes when DST is observed. American English replaces <i>standard</i> with <i>daylight</i>: for example, <i><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Time_Zone" title="Pacific Time Zone">Pacific Standard Time</a></i> (<i>PST</i>) becomes <i>Pacific Daylight Time</i> (<i>PDT</i>). In the United Kingdom, the standard term for UK time when advanced by one hour is <i><a href="/wiki/British_Summer_Time" title="British Summer Time">British Summer Time</a></i> (BST), and British English typically inserts <i>summer</i> into other time zone names, e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Central_European_Time" title="Central European Time">Central European Time</a></i> (<i>CET</i>) becomes <i><a href="/wiki/Central_European_Summer_Time" title="Central European Summer Time">Central European Summer Time</a></i> (<i>CEST</i>).
</p><p>The North American English mnemonic "spring forward, fall back" (also "spring ahead&#160;...", "spring up&#160;...", and "...&#160;fall behind") helps people remember in which direction to shift the clocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Downing-Prerau_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Downing-Prerau-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computing">Computing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Computing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Daylightsavings.svg" class="image"><img alt="Strong man in sandals and with shaggy hair, facing away from audience/artist, grabbing a hand of a clock bigger than he is and attempting to force it backwards. The clock uses Roman numerals and the man is dressed in stripped-down Roman gladiator style. The text says &quot;You can&#39;t stop time... But you can turn it back one hour at 2 a.m. on Oct. 28 when daylight-saving time ends and standard time begins.&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Daylightsavings.svg/170px-Daylightsavings.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Daylightsavings.svg/255px-Daylightsavings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Daylightsavings.svg/340px-Daylightsavings.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="976" data-file-height="1472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Daylightsavings.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 2001 US <a href="/wiki/Public_service_advertising" class="mw-redirect" title="Public service advertising">public service advertisement</a> reminded people to adjust clocks.</div></div></div>
<p>Changes to DST rules cause problems in existing computer installations. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_2005#Change_to_daylight_saving_time" title="Energy Policy Act of 2005">2007 change to DST rules</a> in North America required that many computer systems be upgraded, with the greatest impact on e-mail and calendar programs. The upgrades required a significant effort by corporate <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">information technologists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some applications standardize on UTC to avoid problems with clock shifts and time zone differences.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
Likewise, most modern operating systems internally handle and store all times as UTC and only convert to local time for display.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, even if UTC is used internally, the systems still require external <a href="/wiki/Leap_second" title="Leap second">leap second</a> updates and time zone information to correctly calculate local time as needed. Many systems in use today base their date/time calculations from data derived from the <a href="/wiki/Tz_database" title="Tz database">tz&#160;database</a> also known as zoneinfo.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="IANA_time_zone_database">IANA time zone database</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: IANA time zone database">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tz_database" title="Tz database">tz&#160;database</a> maps a name to the named location's historical and predicted clock shifts. This database is used by many computer software systems, including most <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> operating systems, <a href="/wiki/Java_(Sun)" class="mw-redirect" title="Java (Sun)">Java</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Oracle_RDBMS" class="mw-redirect" title="Oracle RDBMS">Oracle RDBMS</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">HP</a>'s "tztab" database is similar but incompatible.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> When temporal authorities change DST rules, zoneinfo updates are installed as part of ordinary system maintenance. In Unix-like systems the TZ <a href="/wiki/Environment_variable" title="Environment variable">environment variable</a> specifies the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tz_zones_by_name" class="mw-redirect" title="List of tz zones by name">location name</a>, as in <code>TZ=':America/New_York'</code>. In many of those systems there is also a system-wide setting that is applied if the TZ environment variable is not set: this setting is controlled by the contents of the <kbd style="background:#EEEEEE; letter-spacing:0.05em; padding-left:0.25em; padding-right:0.2em;">/etc/localtime</kbd> file, which is usually a <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_link" title="Symbolic link">symbolic link</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hard_link" title="Hard link">hard link</a> to one of the zoneinfo files. Internal time is stored in timezone-independent <a href="/wiki/Unix_time" title="Unix time">Unix time</a>; the TZ is used by each of potentially many simultaneous users and processes to independently localize time display.
</p><p>Older or stripped-down systems may support only the TZ values required by <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">POSIX</a>, which specify at most one start and end rule explicitly in the value. For example, <code>TZ='EST5EDT,M3.2.0/02:00,M11.1.0/02:00'</code> specifies time for the eastern United States starting in 2007. Such a TZ value must be changed whenever DST rules change, and the new value applies to all years, mishandling some older timestamps.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microsoft_Windows">Microsoft Windows</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Microsoft Windows">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" decoding="async" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. The reason given is: information about more recent versions of Windows would be useful.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2020</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As with zoneinfo, a user of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> configures DST by specifying the name of a location, and the operating system then consults a table of rule sets that must be updated when DST rules change. Procedures for specifying the name and updating the table vary with release. Updates are not issued for older versions of Microsoft Windows.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a> supports at most two start and end rules per time zone setting. In a Canadian location observing DST, a single Vista setting supports both 1987–2006 and post-2006 time stamps, but mishandles some older time stamps. Older Microsoft Windows systems usually store only a single start and end rule for each zone, so that the same Canadian setting reliably supports only post-2006 time stamps.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These limitations have caused problems. For example, before 2005, DST in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> varied each year and was skipped some years. <a href="/wiki/Windows_95" title="Windows 95">Windows 95</a> used rules correct for 1995 only, causing problems in later years. In <a href="/wiki/Windows_98" title="Windows 98">Windows 98</a>, Microsoft marked Israel as not having DST, forcing Israeli users to shift their computer clocks manually twice a year. The 2005 <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Daylight_Saving_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Israeli Daylight Saving Law">Israeli Daylight Saving Law</a> established predictable rules using the Jewish calendar but Windows zone files could not represent the rules' dates in a year-independent way. Partial workarounds, which mishandled older time stamps, included manually switching zone files every year<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> and a Microsoft tool that switches zones automatically.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, Israel standardized its daylight saving time according to the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Microsoft Windows keeps the system <a href="/wiki/Real-time_clock" title="Real-time clock">real-time clock</a> in local time. This causes several problems, including compatibility when <a href="/wiki/Multi_boot" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi boot">multi booting</a> with operating systems that set the clock to UTC, and double-adjusting the clock when multi booting different Windows versions, such as with a rescue boot disk. This approach is a problem even in Windows-only systems: there is no support for per-user timezone settings, only a single system-wide setting. In 2008 Microsoft hinted that future versions of Windows will partially support a <a href="/wiki/Windows_registry" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows registry">Windows registry</a> entry <var>RealTimeIsUniversal</var> that had been introduced many years earlier, when <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT" title="Windows NT">Windows NT</a> supported <a href="/wiki/RISC" class="mw-redirect" title="RISC">RISC</a> machines with UTC clocks, but had not been maintained.<sup id="cite_ref-RealTimeIsUniversal_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RealTimeIsUniversal-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Since then at least two fixes related to this feature have been published by Microsoft.<sup id="cite_ref-KB2922223_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KB2922223-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KB2800213_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KB2800213-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a> file system used by recent versions of Windows stores the file with a UTC time stamp, but displays it corrected to local—or seasonal—time. However, the <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">FAT</a> filesystem commonly used on removable devices stores only the local time. Consequently, when a file is copied from the hard disk onto separate media, its time will be set to the current local time. If the time adjustment is changed, the timestamps of the original file and the copy will be different. The same effect can be observed when compressing and uncompressing files with some <a href="/wiki/File_archiver" title="File archiver">file archivers</a>. It is the NTFS file that changes seen time. This effect should be kept in mind when trying to determine if a file is a duplicate of another, although there are other methods of comparing files for equality (such as using a <a href="/wiki/Checksum_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Checksum algorithm">checksum algorithm</a>).  A ready clue is if the time stamps differ by precisely 1 hour.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Permanent_daylight_saving_time">Permanent daylight saving time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Permanent daylight saving time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Permanent_daylight_saving_time_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Permanent daylight saving time in the United States">Permanent daylight saving time in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decree_time" title="Decree time">Decree time in Russia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Summer_time_in_Europe#Future" title="Summer time in Europe">Summer time in Europe §&#160;Future</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Willett_memorial.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A standing stone in a grassy field surrounded by trees. The stone contains a vertical sundial centered on 1 o&#39;clock, and is inscribed &quot;HORAS NON NUMERO NISI ÆSTIVAS&quot; and &quot;SUMMER TIME ACT 1925&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Willett_memorial.JPG/170px-Willett_memorial.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Willett_memorial.JPG/255px-Willett_memorial.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Willett_memorial.JPG/340px-Willett_memorial.JPG 2x" data-file-width="897" data-file-height="1147" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Willett_memorial.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/William_Willett" title="William Willett">William Willett</a> Memorial Sundial in <a href="/wiki/Petts_Wood" title="Petts Wood">Petts Wood</a>, south London, is always on DST.</div></div></div>
<p>A move to permanent daylight saving time (staying on summer hours all year with no time shifts) is sometimes advocated and is currently implemented in some jurisdictions such as <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Time_in_Saskatchewan" title="Time in Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Advocates cite the same advantages as normal DST without the problems associated with the twice yearly time shifts. However, many remain unconvinced of the benefits, citing the same problems and the relatively late sunrises, particularly in winter, that year-round DST entails.<sup id="cite_ref-Handwerk_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handwerk-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Other reasons for a permanent change of time zone could be a result of following the time zone of a neighboring region, political will, or other causes.
</p><p>Under German occupation during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, "Berlin Time" was imposed in the Netherlands, Belgium, Luxembourg and France, and Spain introduced this too. After the war and some hesitations, those countries of the UTC±0 time zone adopted Central European Time as their standard time. During DST, they are 2 hours ahead.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Ahead of what? (April 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> switched to permanent DST from 2011 to 2014, but the move proved unpopular because of the late sunrises in winter, so in 2014 Russia switched permanently back to standard time.<sup id="cite_ref-Russia_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russia-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The United Kingdom and Ireland also experimented with year-round summer time between 1968 and 1971, and put clocks forward by an extra hour during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Legislature" title="Florida Legislature">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_State_Legislature" title="Washington State Legislature">Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_State_Legislature" title="California State Legislature">California</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Legislative_Assembly" title="Oregon Legislative Assembly">Oregon</a> legislatures have all passed bills to enact permanent DST, but the bills require Congressional approval in order to take effect. Maine, Massachusetts, New Hampshire, and Rhode Island have also introduced proposals or commissions to that effect.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Although 26 states have considered making DST permanent, unless Congress changes federal law, states cannot implement permanent DST—states can only opt out of DST, not standard time.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under an EU directive, from 2021 twice-yearly adjustment of clocks will cease. Member states will have the option of observing either standard time or summer time all year round.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Experts in circadian rhythms and sleep caution against permanent Daylight Saving Time, recommending year-round Standard Time as the preferred option for public health and safety.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Globe_and_Mail_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Globe_and_Mail-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Sacramento_Bee_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Sacramento_Bee-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-U_Calgary_News_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-U_Calgary_News-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CBC_News_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBC_News-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_with_permanent_DST">Problems with permanent DST</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Problems with permanent DST">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since daylight saving time creates the illusion of the sun rising and setting one hour later on the clock, but does not add any additional daylight, the already later sunrise times under standard time are pushed an hour later on the clock with daylight saving time. Late sunrise times can become unpopular in the winter months which essentially forces workers and schoolchildren to begin the day in darkness. In 1974 following the enactment of the Emergency Daylight Saving Time Act in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, complaints of children going to school in the dark and working people commuting and starting their work day in pitch darkness during the winter months led to the repeal of the act in October 1974 when standard time was restored until February 23, 1975. In 1976, the United States returned to the schedule set under the <a href="/wiki/Uniform_Time_Act_of_1966" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform Time Act of 1966">Uniform Time Act of 1966</a>. In 1971, year-round daylight time in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> was abandoned after a 3-year experiment because of complaints about winter sunrise times. The same complaints also led to <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> abandoning DST and instituting standard time year round in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-Russia_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russia-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country_and_region">By country and region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daylight_saving_time&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: By country and region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-:0-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:0_93-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:0_93-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:0_93-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFDowning2018" class="citation news">Downing, Michael (March 9, 2018). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.smithsonianmag.com/history/100-years-later-madness-daylight-saving-time-endures-180968435/">"One Hundred Years Later, the Madness of Daylight Saving Time Endures"</a>. <i>Smithsonian</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">March 12,</span> 2018</span>. <q>In fact, the best studies we have prove that Americans use more domestic electricity when they practice daylight saving. Moreover, when we turn off the TV and go to the park or the mall in the evening sunlight, Americans don't walk. We get in our cars and drive. Daylight saving actually increases gasoline consumption, and it's a cynical substitute for genuine energy conservation policy.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Smithsonian&amp;rft.atitle=One+Hundred+Years+Later%2C+the+Madness+of+Daylight+Saving+Time+Endures&amp;rft.date=2018-03-09&amp;rft.aulast=Downing&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.smithsonianmag.com%2Fhistory%2F100-years-later-madness-daylight-saving-time-endures-180968435%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADaylight+saving+time" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r951705291"/></span>
</li>
